<div id="review_2822068497" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2822068497" />
    <a title="Sam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/77846202-sam"><img alt="Sam" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2822068497?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam" href="/user/show/77846202-sam">Sam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2822068497" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13950338422210862286">This was the latest I read while reading Richard Powers' novels in order of publication suggested by Neil from the Mookse and Gripes group and IMO, this is Powers most accessible novel as of the publication date. I also believe Powers managed to close the distance from his characters he had shown in earlier books resulting in a warmer feel for The Time of Our Singing.The plot can be ascertained from many of the other reviews. Basically, it is a family novel based on the relationship of German/Je</span>
  <span id="freeText13950338422210862286" style="display:none">This was the latest I read while reading Richard Powers' novels in order of publication suggested by Neil from the Mookse and Gripes group and IMO, this is Powers most accessible novel as of the publication date. I also believe Powers managed to close the distance from his characters he had shown in earlier books resulting in a warmer feel for The Time of Our Singing.The plot can be ascertained from many of the other reviews. Basically, it is a family novel based on the relationship of German/Jewish male physicist and an African-American female with a love for Classical vocal music who meet at a Marian Anderson concert on the Washington DC Mall in 1939, fall in love, marry, and attempt to raise a family without acknowledging the color barrier present at that time. The story focuses mainly on two sons as they try and break that color barrier as performers in Classical/Western Art music. The secondary theme involves the relativity of time as perceived by a physicist. Powers weaves the two themes while highlighting a history of the period especially in American race relations and civil rights. Powers takes a big risk in appropiating another race/religon for this novel and I am not qualified to comment on his success, belonging to neither the race nor religon appropiated, but I do believe Powers gave this his best shot in trying not to show negative bias. The novel soars for me, not in the themes or serious matters discussed, but rather in Powers descriptions of vocal performances by various characters throughout the novel. Powers manages to descibe these as if he were in attendance and is able to make me wish I were there too. Five stars for this his best since The Gold Bug Variations.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13950338422210862286" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText3033479875472450059" style="display:none">I had a mixed reaction to this book. It engaged me and I loved the use of music in the story and, as a singer, the descriptions of singing and music. The discussions of time and the intercutting of different time periods within the story was not entirely successful. In the end, it felt like a white person's book about race. I'd be curious to read comments by African-Americans about the book.</span>

<div id="review_354042105" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/354042105" />
    <a title="Marne Wilson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10054249-marne-wilson"><img alt="Marne Wilson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557579099p2/10054249.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/354042105?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marne Wilson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marne Wilson" href="/user/show/10054249-marne-wilson">Marne Wilson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780374277826" href="/book/show/1198393.The_Time_of_Our_Singing">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10054249-marne-wilson?shelf=love-stories">love-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10054249-marne-wilson?shelf=power-of-words">power-of-words</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10054249-marne-wilson?shelf=academia">academia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10054249-marne-wilson?shelf=family-secrets">family-secrets</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer354042105" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12450432580611075302">The timeline of this story takes us from Marian Anderson's 1939 concert at the Lincoln Memorial to the 1995 Million Man March. Of course, Powers wouldn't be himself if he told this story in anything resembling chronological order, but never fear, we get all the glory and terror of the 20th century eventually.<br/><br/>I had no idea when I checked this book out from the library last March just how timely it was going to be. I began to read it about a month ago, and I arrived at each horrible milestone (lyn</span>
  <span id="freeText12450432580611075302" style="display:none">The timeline of this story takes us from Marian Anderson's 1939 concert at the Lincoln Memorial to the 1995 Million Man March. Of course, Powers wouldn't be himself if he told this story in anything resembling chronological order, but never fear, we get all the glory and terror of the 20th century eventually.<br /><br />I had no idea when I checked this book out from the library last March just how timely it was going to be. I began to read it about a month ago, and I arrived at each horrible milestone (lynchings, demonstrations, race riots) just when an analog came into our news cycle. I've always had an odd knack for synchronicity between books and life, but this was one of the most powerful and most unexpected yet.<br /><br />There are a few reasons why I gave this book that moved me so much only four stars. One is that it simply goes on too long. I think Powers always tends to do this anyway, but in this book, he as a white author must have felt the need to demonstrate that he had done his research, and it shows. There are sections that seem repetitive and didactic as he struggles to prove all his knowledge of the political situation. This took me out of the story a little bit.<br /><br />The other, I guess, is the writing about music. Power's explications of musical pieces in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23012.The_Gold_Bug_Variations" title="The Gold Bug Variations by Richard Powers" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Gold Bug Variations</a> were exquisite, and the many, many similar passages in this book don't quite measure up. I very rarely felt the need to go to Youtube and listen to a given piece after reading his description. And since these sections didn't work as well for me, I felt they could have been tightened up quite a bit.<br /><br />Since this isn't by a black author, I hesitate to tell you that this is the book to read right now, but I do believe that it is more timely now than it was when it was published in 2003. If you choose to invest the time to read it in the near future, you will be handsomely rewarded.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12450432580611075302" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_116289690" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/116289690" />
    <a title="Gloria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1180733-gloria"><img alt="Gloria" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/116289690?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gloria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gloria" href="/user/show/1180733-gloria">Gloria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer116289690" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9099758984804150682">This book was just so good. Moving, fascinating, complex...manages to weave a compelling family story together with an eerily accurate picture of 20th century U.S. history and race relations. I found myself wishing that this book had been written just after Obama's election, so that Inauguration Day could factored into the framing device of huge, defining moments in Washington (would have preferred that to the Million Man March, which closes the novel). The characters felt real enough that I wan</span>
  <span id="freeText9099758984804150682" style="display:none">This book was just so good. Moving, fascinating, complex...manages to weave a compelling family story together with an eerily accurate picture of 20th century U.S. history and race relations. I found myself wishing that this book had been written just after Obama's election, so that Inauguration Day could factored into the framing device of huge, defining moments in Washington (would have preferred that to the Million Man March, which closes the novel). The characters felt real enough that I wanted to know how they would have reacted to a black president, and to the crazy reactionary fervor that followed.<br /><br />This was such an intelligent look at how race, class, and culture intersects for one mixed-race family. I really related to the protagonist, Joseph. I was fortunate enough to be born 25 years later - late enough to be shocked anew by the recounting of prejudice. But Joseph's sense of belonging to elite culture and yet always being a bit of an outsider, his quest for an authentic identity (which leads him to community work in Oakland!), resonated with me. Plus, this book is kind of soft porn for music lovers. I know just enough about music to sort of follow the obscure references, and to realize how little I really know about music. There aren't too many novels that will appeal equally to sociologists, historians, musicologists, and people who just like a well-done family saga.<br /><br />I guess every once in a while the prose is a little self-indulgent, and at 600+ pages there could have been some editing. Minor quibble, though. For once my co-workers praise in recommending this book was not just hype.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9099758984804150682" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13632109734837171945">If that all good novels are born of imagination and reared in some form of literary mechanics to or not in the service of story telling with or without purpose while we the reader are complicit in meaning. Can one who though outsider tell the tale credibly? Some would say yes in gender but unequivocally no to race. Powers' book goes all out mixing race, gender, ethnicity, times, and spins this decades traversing yarn with his characteristic bent for musical underlay. His narrator one half of a b</span>
  <span id="freeText13632109734837171945" style="display:none">If that all good novels are born of imagination and reared in some form of literary mechanics to or not in the service of story telling with or without purpose while we the reader are complicit in meaning. Can one who though outsider tell the tale credibly? Some would say yes in gender but unequivocally no to race. Powers' book goes all out mixing race, gender, ethnicity, times, and spins this decades traversing yarn with his characteristic bent for musical underlay. His narrator one half of a brother duo of mixed race/ethnicity origin in the tumultuous America of civil rights era. They are black and white and educated and talented and Jewish and searching. Music, classical is their medium, they have destiny written into their every fiber with license to be that which they choose as provided in their stolid musically inclined black and white progenitors who also broke down barriers against the grain of their own histories dating back to slavery. Always to it seems that time, memory, quantum theory and whether relativity is relative to now if now is now. This gob of gibberish shouldn't deflect what is another good book of ideas and depictions of lives that this reader came to find empathy for and maybe even hone a few ongoing alterations about beliefs/bias in favor of inclusion. I learn and grow with grace as guide - this book delivers some of both. </span>

<div id="review_156151708" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156151708" />
    <a title="Caroline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/293178-caroline"><img alt="Caroline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1300789973p2/293178.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/156151708?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline" href="/user/show/293178-caroline">Caroline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156151708" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17013861013374071312">I've had this book for a good few years now and I've never before been able to get into it. I've picked it up, read a few pages, put it down again and found something else. And now I wish I'd stayed with it, now I wish I'd read it years ago so I could have read it again and again by now, because...what a book. What a book.<br/><br/>On a purely surface level it's about two mixed-race brothers born in the late 1940s, children to a German Jewish father and a black mother. But it's so much more than that. It'</span>
  <span id="freeText17013861013374071312" style="display:none">I've had this book for a good few years now and I've never before been able to get into it. I've picked it up, read a few pages, put it down again and found something else. And now I wish I'd stayed with it, now I wish I'd read it years ago so I could have read it again and again by now, because...what a book. What a book.<br /><br />On a purely surface level it's about two mixed-race brothers born in the late 1940s, children to a German Jewish father and a black mother. But it's so much more than that. It's about identity and birthright, about time and music, racism and relativity and what it means to be human. It's been described as 'one of the greatest American novels ever written' and I couldn't agree more. It's incredible.<br /><br />The language alone would make it all worthwhile - this man has a gift with words and imagery that rivals Pat Conroy, and the latter is one of my favourite authors. And it's packed full of scientific metaphors, references to art and history and music, many of which I have to confess went sailing over my head but didn't lessen my enjoyment one iota.<br /><br />It's not an undemanding read but oh, it's worth it. Every page is a treasure. I was only sorry to come to the end of it, and I know it's a book I'll return to. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17013861013374071312" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Xcy99r99Br2y4jADJ/vJYzpysuiXSDATsdMZPJlsqsXDx+QOIR8vURUYEpk6QMEMcAoCUAetRuEQE89+RfDG1A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('E7p4vQOLyNFn0gSWQduHZc7CHBU33/bUGeNcZ5NHqwaNsSFFnenhPcAoJgxcYI8KhLqsrac6gCa4I4olT9vHFw==')})
  }

//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_52049749'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52049749-la-panth-re-des-neiges?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">La Panthère des neiges<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/810151.Sylvain_Tesson?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Sylvain Tesson<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.80 avg rating &mdash; 942 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2019\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer2614773913155294559\">En 2018, Sylvain Tesson est invité par le photographe animalier Vincent Munier à observer aux confins du Tibet les derniers spécimens de la panthère des neiges. Ces animaux discrets et très craintifs vivent sur un gigantesque plateau culminant à 5 00<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2614773913155294559\" style=\"display:none\">En 2018, Sylvain Tesson est invité par le photographe animalier Vincent Munier à observer aux confins du Tibet les derniers spécimens de la panthère des neiges. Ces animaux discrets et très craintifs vivent sur un gigantesque plateau culminant à 5 000 m d\'altitude, le Changtang. Situé au Tibet septentrional et occidental, il s\'étend sur environ 1 600 km, du Ladakh à la province du Qinghai, et il est habité par les nomades Changpas.\nL\'équipe atterrit à Pékin, puis prend la route à bord d\'un 4X4 en direction du Tibet. Au fil des jours le convoi s\'achemine vers des panoramas de plus en plus grandioses et déserts : là où la population recule, la faune avance et se déploie, protégée des effets nocifs de la civilisation.\nSylvain Tesson décrit une sorte de savane africaine qui serait perchée à 4 000 mètres d\'altitude, où l\'on croise des troupeaux d\'antilopes, des chèvres bleues, des hordes de yacks qui traversent de vastes étendues herbeuses où s\'élèvent des dunes.\nL\'équipe s\'enfonce toujours plus loin, se hissant à des hauteurs qui dépassent largement ce que nous connaissons en Europe. À 5 000 m d\'altitude s\'ouvre le domaine de la panthère des neiges. Dans ce sanctuaire naturel totalement inhospitalier pour l\'homme, le félin a trouvé les moyens de sa survie et de sa tranquillité. Les conditions d\'observation deviennent très difficiles, il faut parfois rester immobile pendant trente heures consécutives par -30° C pour apercevoir quelques minutes le passage majestueux de l\'animal...\nSylvain Tesson entrecroise habilement le récit d\'une aventure exceptionnelle aux confins du Tibet avec des réflexions d\'une pertinence remarquable sur les conséquences désastreuses de l\'activité humaine envers le règne animal. À travers l\'exemple de la panthère des neiges, l\'auteur s\'interroge sur la morphologie d\'un monde où toutes les espèces viendraient à se raréfier puis à s\'éteindre. Il nous entraîne dans cette aventure singulière où l\'on s\'intéresse autant à l\'art de l\'affût animalier qu\'à la spiritualité asiatique.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"2614773913155294559\" What do we mean when we use the term? And can it ever truly exist? Astra Taylor, hailed as a &quot;New Civil Rights Leader&quot; (LA Times), provides surprising answers.There is no shortage of democracy, at least in name, and yet it is in crisis everywhere we look. From a cabal of thieving plutocrats in the White House to rising inequality and xenophobia worldwide, it is clear that democracy--specifically the principle of government by and for the people--is not living up to its promise.\n\nIn Democracy May Not Exist, but We\'ll Miss It When It\'s Gone, Astra Taylor shows that real democracy--fully inclusive and completely egalitarian--has in fact never existed. In a tone that is both philosophical and anecdotal, weaving together history, theory, the stories of individuals, and conversations with such leading thinkers as Cornel West, Danielle Allen, and Wendy Brown, Taylor invites us to reexamine the term. Is democracy a means or an end, a process or a set of desired outcomes? What if the those outcomes, whatever they may be--peace, prosperity, equality, liberty, an engaged citizenry--can be achieved by non-democratic means? Or if an election leads to a terrible outcome? If democracy means rule by the people, what does it mean to rule and who counts as the people?\n\nThe inherent paradoxes are too often unnamed and unrecognized. By teasing them out, Democracy May Not Exist, but We\'ll Miss It When It\'s Gone offers a better understanding of what is possible, what we want, and why democracy is so hard to realize.<\/span>\n  <a <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6662435018437372948"><strong>Richard Powers</strong> is the author of twelve novels, most recently <em>The Overstory</em>. He is the recipient of a MacArthur Fellowship and the National Book Award, and he has been a Pulitzer Prize and four-time National Book Critics Circle Award finalist. He lives in the foothills of the Great Smoky Mountains.<br/><br/> <i>Librarian note: There is more than one author with this name in the Goodreads database. </i></span>
